Secondly, the solutions to making the decision‐making process more effectual must be sustainable. Institutional reforms must address the needs of the Union no matter how many new members are admitted in the future and regardless of the number of phases involved.

Thirdly, decisions on the new reforms must be taken in good time and before the next round of enlargement. Finland will be ready for its own part to move the reform process on as soon as the preconditions for doing so are in place. The historical decision on EMU has been taken and EMU will come into effect at the start of next year. Now the ratification of the Treaty of Amsterdam and the proposals of Agenda 2000 have to be decided on. As things stand, the time could be ripe for starting the institutional reform process at the end of next year.

More also is expected at international level of a Union as it is enlarged and becomes more integrated. The EU has influential power and it bears a responsibility that extends beyond its borders. The Union"s negotiating powers have been considerable, particularly in traditional trade policy negotiations. But overall the Union"s international effectiveness does not reflect its economic importance.

For the Union to be able to monitor its interests and make a constructive contribution to the resolution of common problems, it must step up its activity in the area of external relations. This includes economic external relations, where the birth of the single currency area will highlight the need, more than ever before, to adopt Community practice on a comprehensive basis.

In addition, more will be expected of the EU in resolving international crises. It was written into the Treaty of Amsterdam that the EU should become more effective in military crisis control. This would meet the security needs that exist in Europe and would also be a realistic step, in the light of current developments, towards a common defence policy. With the Treaty having come into force, the Union should have the political will to intervene in crises – by military means also if necessary.

(Applause)

In the area of crisis control the EU should be the most versatile player there is, as it also has economic and political tools available to it.

Only an effective Union that handles its external relations as one body can function in accordance with a new, broader‐based approach to security.

Such new challenges for the EU have also come from northern European regions. For example, Finland"s eastern frontier, in other words, the border between the EU and Russia, is one of the world"s most gaping chasms between different standards of living. Behind this, and elsewhere in northern Europe, numerous other problems smoulder, and their resolution is just as much in the interest of us all. On the other hand, there is great potential in these regions at pan‐European level, which can be effectively exploited only through cooperation between states.

For this reason, the Union must create a northern dimension, by means of which it can coordinate and structure its activity in Northern Europe. In recent years, the EU has strengthened its southern dimension successfully through the Barcelona process. Both the northern and southern dimensions are bases for successfully creating stability and security in regions adjacent to the EU and strengthening the role of the Union as an international player.

European integration was born out of war, rose out of the ashes, and grew in the spirit of solidarity. Its power rests in its freedom of choice and common values. Politically, the cohesive force of integration lies in the European Union and its institutions, which guarantee continuity.

Changes in Europe will continue to take place. Political decision‐making has to adjust to the demands that change brings with it. But mere adjustment is not enough. The legacy of past generations and our children"s future imposes far more obligations on us.

Fifty years ago the founders of the European communities proposed a view of a new Europe. That view has at long last been realized. Now Europe needs a new vision for a new millennium and decision‐makers who have the courage to trust in the power of integration.

(Loud sustained applause)

  President . – Thank you very much, Mr Ahtisaari.

(The formal sitting was closed at 1.00 p.m.)

ANNEX

 

1. Questions to the Council

 

Question No 21 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0546/98)

Subject: EU telecommunications surveillance system

A STOA report entitled 'An appraisal of technologies of political control' (PE 166.499) describes US and UK telecommunications surveillance systems and states that the EU, too, cooperates with them.

Does the Council consider the STOA report to provide an accurate account of the situation, and does cooperation by the EU also involve Echolon?

Answer

It is not within the competence of the Council to comment.

Question No 22 by Jan Andersson (H−0549/98)

Subject: Protection of workers' health and safety

The Advocate‐General has delivered his opinion in the case of Società italiana petroli SpA (IP) vs Borsana Srl (No. C−2/97) in regard to paragraph 118a of the EC Treaty concerning protection of workers' health and safety.

If the opinion of the Advocate‐General also becomes the Court's judgment, it may have serious implications as the Member States will be prevented from setting higher standards than those established in the directives.

Does the Council take the same view as the Advocate‐General in this case? If not, what measures does it intend to take?

Answer

The honourable Member will understand that it is not the Council's role to express an opinion on conclusions formulated by the Advocate‐General of the Court of Justice.

Question No 23 by Inger Schörling (H−0553/98)

Subject: Construction of motorway in Poland under the TEN

Through the European Investment Bank and the PHARE programme, the Union is funding the construction of a motorway through the Mount St Anna's Landscape Park in Poland. There are five nature reserves within the park, which is protected by Polish environment laws, but the regional authorities responsible for the area have now decided that a strip of land in the centre of the park, where the plans show that the motorway is to be built, is no longer to form part of the landscape park. This method of circumventing regulations has been criticized by Polish environment organizations, among others, both on environmental and legal grounds. The motorway, which forms part of the EU's trans‐European networks project, will severely affect the Mount St Anna's Landscape Park

Does the Council consider that it is reasonable to set different environmental criteria for new projects in the EU Member States and EU‐funded projects in the applicant countries and the rest of the world? In the Council's view, what responsibility does it have for ensuring that EU funds are not given to environmentally damaging projects in third countries?

Answer

The Council is conscious of the importance of environment protection, both in the European Union and in partner countries. It has therefore always put an emphasis on the need to properly assess the environmental impact of investment projects beneficiaries of EU funding.

The Accession Partnership with Poland, of which the Council has set the principles, priorities and objectives and which form the new policy guidelines for management of the PHARE programme in Poland, includes an important chapter on environment.

Concerning, in particular, the case of the A4 motorway between Wraclaw and Katowice, the Council is informed that the laying out of the project was approved in the early 90s after local public consultations and that measures have been taken to protect the environment in the field of water, noise and the specific vegetation of the area. Further consultations are being held at the moment, the last of them having taken place earlier this month, between the local provincial government and a Polish environmental grouping.

The Council thanks the honourable Member for her question and shares the opinion that specific importance should be attached to the protection of the Mount St Anna's Landscape Park. It will draw the Commission's attention to this necessity, as this institution is competent for management of the PHARE programme in application of Regulation 3906/89 of the Council. It will do the same with the EIB.

The Council furthermore considers that, as the Polish authorities have the main responsibility in establishing environmentally sound infrastructure projects in their country, it could be useful to address the subject during the Joint Parliamentary Committee which this Assembly will hold with its Polish counterpart next week (22‐24.06.98).

Question No 24 by Alex Smith (H−0554/98)

Subject: Future trade relations with ACP States

It is important that ACP countries are offered viable options for their future trade relations with the EU. In this light, could the President‐in‐Office outline the steps he would like to see taken to ensure that the EU's GSP system is upgraded so it can become viable alternative for ACP States? Does the President‐in‐Office feel an upgraded GSP scheme could offer all poor ACP countries (not just LDCs) market access equivalent to that available under the current Lomé trade arrangements?

Does the President‐in‐Office support the view expressed by Professor Pinheiro that in order to establish the proposed regional FTA's with ACP regions, WTO rules on FTAs will need to be changed and made more flexible in favour of developing countries? Does the Presidency envisage an EU initiative within the WTO to make such changes possible?

Answer

The General Affairs Council in March concluded that:

‐ The new trade arrangements would be one element of a comprehensive and integrated approach for a strengthened ACP/EC partnership based on political dialogue, development assistance and trade. The trade chapter in the new Convention should be coherent with the other provisions of the Convention. The ultimate objective is the progressive integration of the ACP States into the world economy and their full participation in a liberalized market trading system.

‐ These arrangements should at least maintain the current market access for ACPs and must be WTO‐compatible.

‐ Further work should be done on agreements on region or country‐based Free Trade Areas which should be compatible with wider EU trade policy objectives to promote multilateral trade liberalization.

‐ The Council will consider alternatives. This could include enhancement of the GSP.

‐ The trade regime for LDCs should be improved in accordance with the Council Conclusions of 1 June 1997.

The General Affairs Council on 8 June discussed this issue.

Discussions will continue in Coreper with a view to reaching agreement on the EU's negotiating mandate at the General Affairs Council on 29 June.

The EU and its Member States are active members of the WTO. They participate constructively in its development and have undertaken to fully respect its rules. There are no plans for an EU initiative to make the changes suggested by the honourable Member.

Question No 25 by Undine‐Uta Bloch von Blottnitz (H−0561/98)

Subject: Contamination connected with the transport of nuclear waste

For at least ten years now, parts of the European nuclear industry have been informed about enormous radiological problems connected with the transport of nuclear waste within Europe. The limit values laid down have been exceeded by a factor of up to 3000. The Member State supervisory bodies claim that they received no information until early May 1998.

What practical measures does the Council believe are necessary or have already been considered in order to ensure that the limit values laid down in respect of the transport of nuclear waste are not exceeded and that the consequent health hazards are prevented?

Answer

Council Directive 96/29/Euratom of 13 May 1996 laying down basic safety standards for the protection of the health of workers and the general public against the dangers arising from ionizing radiation applies to all practices which involve a risk from ionizing radiation including transport of radioactive waste. Moreover, Council Directive 94/55/EC of 21 November 1994 and Council Directive 96/49/EC of 23 July 1996 lay down specific provisions on the approximation of the laws of the Member States with regard to the transport of dangerous goods by road and by rail, respectively.

It is the responsibility of the Member States to bring into force the laws, regulations and administrative provisions necessary to comply with these Directives in due time. The Commission is forthwith informed and receives the national texts adopted in the field governed by each Directive

Question No 26 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0563/98)

Subject: COM for raw tobacco and women's employment

When taking a decision on the reform of the COM in the raw tobacco sector, will the Council take into account the economic and social importance of that crop for women's employment?

Answer

The Commission has submitted a proposal for a Council Regulation amending the current Regulation on the Common Organization of the Market in raw tobacco.

The proposal is presently under examination by the Council with a view to its adoption if possible later this month. In the framework of this examination all relevant aspects, including the consequences on employment in general and on socio‐economic structures, will be duly taken into account.

The Commission proposal is based on Article 43 of the Treaty. Therefore, the European Parliament will provide its opinion on it. This opinion is expected to be delivered at this present part‐session, and therefore in time for the Council to act next week. I am sure that the specific aspect of women's employment which the honourable Member has addressed in her question will form part of this opinion.

When taking its formal decision, the Council will naturally take due account of all the elements contained in the opinion of the European Parliament.

Question No 27 by Bernd Posselt (H−0566/98)

Subject: Return to 'Republika Srpska'

According to the Council's information, how many non‐Serbian displaced persons have so far returned to 'Republika Srpska' in Bosnia‐Herzegovina, and what action is the Council planning to take to accelerate this process?

Answer

The Council has no detailed information on the number of non‐Serbian displaced persons who have returned to Republika Srpska. However, it is clear that the return of minorities to their places of origin remains a major problem, not just in Republika Srpska, but also in the Federation and in neighbouring Croatia. This three‐way or all‐way return is vital for the future peace and stability of the region as a whole. The Council has made clear both in its public declarations and in its diplomatic contacts with the authorities concerned that it expects all the countries of the region to play their part in enabling and assisting minority populations to return to their homes. The arrival of the Dodik Government in Republika Srpska has changed the climate, but much remains to be done. In this context, the elections throughout Bosnia and Herzegovina in September will be very important for the future well‐being and security of all refugees and displaced persons.

The Union was a major player in the Sarajevo Returns Conference in February and the Banja Luka Regional Returns Conference in April. The latter owes much to a Union initiative. While these conferences were a step forward, progress still remains insufficient. In this regard, concrete action by Croatia in favour of the return of Serb refugees to the Krajina is essential to the whole return process.

The Union will continue to urge all the parties to live up to their obligations under the Dayton/Paris peace agreements and will continue to work closely with the Office of the High Representative, as well as the UNHCR and other concerned organisations.

Question No 28 by Anne McIntosh (H−0569/98)

Subject: Common Fisheries Policy

Will the Council of Ministers confirm that the 6/12 mile limits contained in the present Common Fisheries Policy will be maintained beyond 2002?

Answer

As the honourable Member is aware, Article 6 of the so‐called basic Regulation, that is Regulation No 3760/92 establishing a Community system for fisheries and aquaculture, lays down that Member States are authorized to retain access restrictions within the 12 nautical mile limit until 31 December 2002.

Article 14 paragraph 2 of the same regulation also stipulates that the Commission shall present a report to Parliament and Council before 31 December 2001 on the fisheries situation in the Community and on the implementation of this regulation. On the basis of that report the Council shall decide by 31 December the following year on any necessary adjustments be made.

In its report to Parliament and Council of July 1996 on the application of the Community system for fisheries and aquaculture the Commission already drew attention to the 2002 deadline for the 12‐mile limit and indicated that the Commission ‘will make proposals to the Council for provisions to replace the present rules’. The Commission also states ‘it seems unlikely, given the current state of affairs, that there will be a desire to modify’ the present rules.

The Commission's preparatory work on the review is under way and the Council was given an oral report on the state of play at its meeting on 8 June. However, it is not for the Council to prejudge at this stage any proposals which the Commission may consider appropriate to submit in the context of that review. The Council has, of course, noted Parliament's view, expressed in its resolution of last June, that the 12‐mile limit should continue to be for the exclusive use and management of the coastal Member State concerned.

Question No 29 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0571/98)

Subject: Digital television

Digital television offers considerable advantages for the future of television and electronic information highways and it is, therefore, important that the EU has a consistent and comprehensive strategy for the development of this technology and the standards for the new digital systems.

Does the Council, therefore, intend to set up a legal framework for digital television? Is European industry ready to meet the demands of this major new market which is emerging and are negotiations under way with Japan and the USA in this sector?

Answer

The Council is fully aware of the advantages of digital television and would like to remind the honourable Member, that on 24 October 1995 the European Parliament and the Council, adopted Directive 95/47/EC on the use of standards for the transmission of television signals, which requires the use of standardised transmission systems for digital television.

This Directive contains a recital based on an amendment by the European Parliament which urged the introduction and enforcement of efficient anti‐piracy legislation at European level. In response to this concern the Commission proposed a Directive of the European Parliament and of the Council on the legal protection of services based on, or consisting of, conditional access. The Council rapidly reached agreement in principle on a common position on this Directive on 18 May 1998 and formal adoption of the common position is expected later this month.

As regards further development of the legal framework for digital television it is to be noted that the Council has not yet received a new proposal from the Commission. However, it is understood that the Commission may propose to update Directive 95/47/EC in the near future, in the light of technological developments, taking into account in particular the outcome of the debate on the Green Paper on the Convergence of the telecommunications, media and information technology sectors.

Question No 30 by Laura González Álvarez (H−0572/98)

Subject: Agreement of 18 May 1998 between the EU and the USA on the extraterritorial sanctions laid down in the Helms‐Burton (Cuba) and ILSA (Libya/Iran) laws

Since the EU has undertaken to inhibit and discourage future European investment in assets expropriated in Cuba, can the Council say whether it considers:

That the agreement gives provisions laid down unilaterally by the USA extraterritorial validity;

That the agreement will seriously jeopardize the interests of European investors in Cuba;

That the EU must apply this agreement even if the US Congress refuses to play its part;

That the agreement undermines the national sovereignty of the Member States and weakens the EU's negotiating position in the MAI and the WTO?

Answer

The outcome of the EU‐US Summit in London on 18 May offers the prospect of resolving differences over extra‐territorial sanctions which have cast a shadow over the transatlantic relationship.

The US Administration will now have to obtain from Congress certain amendments to the Helms‐Burton Act. In a unilateral statement which forms part of the package the EU made its commitments dependent on the effective granting of waivers to EU companies and individuals from the provisions of Helms‐Burton and ILSA. In its statement the EU has also reiterated and confirmed its strong opposition as a matter of law and principle to secondary boycott legislation with extra‐territorial effect and retroactivity. The US Administration has undertaken to resist such legislation in future.

One of the elements agreed on 18 May is the ‘Understanding with Respect to Disciplines for the Strengthening of Investment Protection’. The Understanding on Disciplines – which constitutes a political arrangement between the EU and the US – provides inter alia for the denial of government support and commercial assistance for certain transactions on or after 18 May 1998 related to property lawfully expropriated in contravention of international law. Decisions whether a property has been so expropriated will be taken on a case‐by‐case basis. Beyond this the only undertaking is to prevent, subject to limitations under national law, the exploitation of property illegally expropriated in the future. National sovereignty is no more undermined by these EU‐US arrangements that it is by any negotiated undertakings between countries; nor do they weaken the EU's negotiating position in the MAI and the WTO.

On the basis of this package with all its elements, European investors will have greater confidence about doing business abroad in normal conditions, and in accordance with international law.

Question No 31 by Nuala Ahern (H−0574/98)

Subject: The safe transport of radioactive material in the European Union

On 21 April 1998 the Commission forwarded to the Council a report by its Standing Working Group on the Safe Transport of Radioactive Material in the European Union (COM(98) 155 final). What are the views of the Council on its conclusions in the light of the banning of the further rail transport of radioactive waste and irradiated nuclear fuels from, to and within France, Germany and the UK, within three weeks of its publication? And does the Council retain its confidence in the competence of the Standing Working Group?

Answer

The Council attaches great importance to the issue of the safe transport of radioactive material.

In this context, it welcomed the presentation, by the Commission, of its communication concerning the report of the Standing Working Group on the safe transport of radioactive material in the European Union.

This presentation was followed by a preliminary exchange of views within the competent Council Working Party at the end of May 1998.

Since an in‐depth examination has not yet bee completed, it would be premature for the Council to express a final position on the matter. However, the Council wishes to assure the honourable Member of Parliament that any conclusions on this subject to which it may come will be made known in due course.

As regards the suspension of rail transport of the material in question to which the honourable Member refers, this action by the governments of certain Member States, acting within their competence, is not one on which the Council takes position.

Question No 32 by Brian Crowley (H−0575/98)

Subject: Counterfeit football shirts

What new initiatives does the Council consider should be taken to tackle the trade in counterfeit World Cup football shirts which are turned out in British and Chinese sweatshops and furthermore, what action should the EU take to stamp out the market for counterfeit products which in the UK alone is estimated to be worth £1 billion a year with fake clothing and sportswear said to be growing particularly fast and has the Council assessed this criminal activity at an EU level and, if so, what are its conclusions and, finally, does the Council agree that part of the solution to the problem lies in the fact that the cost of football shirts is too high and should be reduced?

Answer

The Council points out that Regulation (EC) No 3295/94 enables the customs authorities to suspend the carrying out of an import, export or transit operation where the goods concerned are suspected of being counterfeit or pirated versions of goods which are covered by an intellectual property right.

In January 1998 the Commission submitted to the Council, on the one hand, a report on the application of the said Regulation, which concerns in particular the outcome of border controls carried out pursuant thereto and, on the other hand, a proposal amending the above mentioned Regulation No 3295/95 with a view to extending its scope to include the protection of patents and of the Community trade mark.

Question No 33 by Niall Andrews (H−0576/98)

Subject: Mafia trafficking in immigrants

There is growing evidence of a continuing trafficking of immigrants into the EU from third countries. The life savings of potential illegal refugees are paid over to Mafia‐style gangs who are evidently using European networks to smuggle illegal immigrants into different EU States. To what extent have EU Member States made trafficking in immigrants a crime and what new initiatives does the Council intend to put forward to halt such deplorable criminal acts?

Answer

1.Although the Council has not compiled an inventory of national legislations of Member States specifically addressing the question of trafficking in immigrants, it is aware of the serious problems posed by this increasingly worrying phenomenon, and has taken a number of initiatives aimed at putting a stop to it.

2.In view of the various dimensions of this phenomenon, the Council has taken action at different levels, including the issue raised by the Honourable Member of the extent to which trafficking is considered an offence, but also covering other areas, such as migration policy and judicial and police cooperation.

3.On 24 February 1997, the Council adopted a Joint Action on the basis of Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, concerning action to combat trafficking in human beings and sexual exploitation of children (OJ No L 63 of 4.3.1997). This Joint Action requires Member States to take specific action at national level to make trafficking a punishable offence under national law. It also establishes forms of judicial cooperation in the investigations and judicial processes relating to trafficking.

4.Prior to that, on 29 November 1996, the Council adopted a Joint Action also on the basis of Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, establishing an incentive and exchange programme for persons responsible for combating trade in human beings and the sexual exploitation of children (Programme STOP (OJ L 322 of 12.12.1996). Partly on the basis of this Programme, in April 1998 the EU launched an information campaign, together with the United States, to combat trafficking in women for the purposes of sexual exploitation. The United States took the lead on an information campaign organized in the Ukraine, and the EU has the lead on the information campaign in Poland.

5.On 16 December 1996, the Council adopted a further Joint Action on the basis of Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, extending the mandate given to the Europol Drugs Unit (EDU) to cover, inter alia, the exchange of information and intelligence in relation to crimes involving clandestine immigration networks and traffic in human beings (OJ No L 342 of 31.12.1996). On the basis of this extended mandate, in particular, EDU is contributing to the tackling of the involvement of organized crime in the influx of migrants from Iraq and the neighbouring region, in accordance with the EU Action Plan adopted by the Council on 26 January 1998.

6.In addition: the Council is following with great attention the developments taking place in this matter within the United Nations, specifically the initiatives aiming at the conclusion of universal instruments against trafficking in children or trafficking in illegal immigrants: The Council is coordinating the positions of Member States with a view to a rapid successful outcome of these initiatives.

Question No 34 by Richard Howitt (H−0583/98)

Subject: Presidency initiative on urban needs in Europe

Following on from the specific agenda item tabled by the presidency at the Informal Council of Ministers in Glasgow on 8 and 9 June 1998, what decisions have been taken with regard to new urban initiatives in the European Union? When does the Council believe there will be a fully‐fledged European Urban policy?

Answer

1.Informal meetings of Ministers are held so that Ministers can engage in exchanges of views that are as free as possible. The very nature of these meetings means that they have no agenda as such.

2.Representatives of the European Parliament were invited to take part in the meeting on 8 June, during which Ministers held an exchange of views on urban issues.

3.Regarding a more substantial European urban policy, it should be noted that the Treaty grants the Union no competence in this connection. Nevertheless, the Structural Funds have provided aid for urban development, through either regional or national programmes or within the framework of Community initiatives such as URBAN.

Question No 35 by Pat Gallagher (H−0585/98)

Subject: Fraud and the EURO

Can the Council outline the measures which will be put in place to ensure that counterfeit fraud will not be possible with the new EURO currency?

Answer

In its regulation of 3 May 1998 on denominations and technical specifications of euro coins intended for circulation, the Council laid down special security features to reduce the scope for counterfeiting the one and two euro coins, given their high value.

The ECB, which has the exclusive right to authorize the issue of euro bank notes, will undoubtedly see to it that the design of these bank notes also contains the necessary safety features.

Question No 37 by Liam Hyland (H−0587/98)

Subject: Rural development

Will the Council outline the progress it has made in promoting a European rural development policy during the term‐in‐office of the British Presidency?

Answer

After receiving the Commission's proposals for Agenda 2000 on 18 March, and following an open debate which took place at the Agriculture Council meeting on 31 March 1998 on the proposals for reform of the CAP, the Council's subordinate bodies began a thorough examination of the Commission's proposals for reform of the CAP, including a proposal concerning support for rural development by the EAGGF.

As a result of these discussions the Agriculture Council on 25 and 26 May 1998 was able to reach some initial conclusions on the proposals for reform of the CAP as a whole by identifying a number of major issues which will be at the core of its future discussions and by defining the framework in which intensive negotiations will be conducted.

The promotion of the development of the rural economy and the rural environment, including multi functional agriculture, the degree of subsidiarity and simplification appropriate to these measures, and the extent of the mechanisms and resources required for this purpose, is one of the major issues identified. This means that the important role to be played by rural development policy, in connection with reform of the CAP, is clearly acknowledged.

The Parliament has already received the report addressed to the Cardiff European Council on Agenda 2000 which gives the Honourable Members a more detailed view of the progress achieved on Agenda 2000, and in particular on rural development.

Question No 38 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0590/98)

Subject: Threats against Cyprus

A great many statements have been made recently by EU and Member States' officials concerning the deployment of S300 missiles in Cyprus. Press reports claim that a scenario is building up which will result in hostilities, response and evacuation of the communities. It is also claimed that, in response to this situation, some Member States have set up an ad hoc group whose aim is to obstruct any further progress of negotiations for the accession of Cyprus.

Does the Council not consider that it is the inalienable right of the Republic of Cyprus to organize its defence and select its defence systems and that any interference constitutes an infringement of the national sovereignty of an independent state, legitimizes and justifies the Turkish threats and runs counter to Community decisions?

Answer

It is the right of the Republic of Cyprus to organize its defence. However, the Council calls on the parties concerned to refrain from any action that might increase tensions in the region. The Council underlines that the United Nations Security Council has stressed in its Resolution 1146 of 23 December 1997 the importance of eventual demilitarization of the Republic of Cyprus as an objective in the context of an overall comprehensive settlement.

For the European Union, the best way to contribute to an alleviation of the situation is to continue efforts to find a political solution to the Cyprus question. The Council reaffirms its full support for the search for such a solution through the talks under the aegis of the United Nations with a view to creating a bi‐communal, bi‐zonal federation as referred to in numerous UN Security Council resolutions. The Council hopes that the talks will resume without delay.

Regarding the negotiations with the accession of Cyprus to the EU, opened on 31 March 1998, the Council believes that they will contribute positively to the search for a political solution to the Cyprus question. The accession of Cyprus to the EU should benefit all communities and help to bring about civil peace and reconciliation on the island. The Council is not aware of any ad hoc group aiming at obstructing further progress on accession negotiations. The European Council at Luxembourg agreed that each of the applicant states will proceed at its own rate, depending on its degree of preparedness.

The Luxembourg European Council has requested that the willingness of the Government of Cyprus to include representatives of the Turkish Cypriot community in the accession negotiating delegation be acted upon. The necessary contacts are being undertaken by the Presidency and the Commission.

Question No 39 by James Nicholson (H−0591/98)

Subject: The UK 'Born‐After' Date Export Scheme

Will the Council please provide us with an update as to the progress that has been made towards approving the United Kingdom's 'Born‐After' Data Export Scheme, which was submitted to the Commission in October 1997?

Answer

The European Council in Florence agreed on the criteria and procedures to be followed for a step‐by‐step relaxation of the embargo on the export of beef from the United Kingdom to the rest of the European Union and to third countries.

In the context of the framework established, the European Council:

‐ invited the Commission to present the appropriate decisions when it considered that the necessary conditions, based on scientific and technical advice , have been fulfilled;

‐ considered that future decisions on the relaxation of current restrictions will be taken only and exclusively on the basis of public health and objective scientific criteria and of the judgement of the Commission, in accordance with the existing procedures, that this criteria has been satisfied.

From this, it is clear that two conditions have to be met, namely: a positive scientific and technical advice and a judgement by the Commission that, on this basis, the appropriate conditions have been satisfied.

In accordance with the criteria and procedures laid down by the European Council in Florence, the Council has already, acting on a proposal from the Commission, adopted, on 16 March 1998, Decision 98/256/EC the aim of which was to relax conditions on beef from Northern Ireland originated from certified herds (export certified herds scheme).

This Decision entered into force on 1 June 1998.

Acting always in the framework of the Florence Agreement, the Commission considered that the conditions were met to submit a new proposal for a further relaxation of the UK ban, taking into consideration the Date‐based Export Scheme that the UK had proposed to the Commission in accordance with the guidelines and conditions set out by the Scientific Steering Committee.

The proposal sets out the rules for a future partial lifting of the ban on the export of beef and beef products under this scheme.

To summarize, this scheme will allow the dispatch, under certain criteria, of deboned fresh meat and products thereof from eligible animals born and reared in the United Kingdom after 1 August 1996, the date on which the meat and bonemeal ban was put in place and properly enforced. Bovine animals are only eligible for export provided that all records of their lifetime and movements are recorded in the animals' official computerized identification and tracing system and if the animals are more than 6 months but less than 30 months of age. To address the risk of maternal transmission, evidence must be provided that the dam of the eligible animals has lived for at least 6 months after the birth and has not contracted BSE. A supplementary condition for the DBES is the slaughtering of all off‐spring born after 1 August 1996 of dams in which BSE has been confirmed.

Finally, before the partial lifting of the ban might become effective, a control mission by the Food and Veterinary Office will have to be carried out on all aspects of the Date‐based Export Scheme (DBES) put in place by the United Kingdom.

The Commission proposal is presently under consideration within the Standing Veterinary Committee. Should, however, this committee not be able to deliver an opinion, then the proposal will be submitted by the Commission to Council.

Question No 40 by Mark Killilea (H−0592/98)

Subject: Disadvantaged areas and milk quota

Has the Council examined the Commission's proposals to distribute an additional 1 % milk quota to some disadvantaged areas such as Nordic regions and mountain regions but to exclude other disadvantaged areas from a share of this additional quota – notably those farmers who operate in marginal land, already classified as disadvantaged in countries such as Ireland and Britain – and does the Council take the view that all disadvantaged areas should be treated on an equitable basis with regard to the distribution of additional milk quota?

Answer

The Council has already held an initial extensive and in‐depth examination of the Commission proposals on agriculture within Agenda 2000, including on the dairy sector.

In the dairy sector, the Commission proposes a 15 % price cut in institutional prices, a partial compensation for this cut through direct aids and an increase in overall milk quota of 2 % with priority allocated for young farmers (1 %) and producers in mountain areas (1 %).

The 25 May Agriculture Council agreed conclusions which identified some key issues which will be central to future work. The role of production controls within the reformed CAP, for example milk quotas, is one of those key issues.

The Council has also agreed that the objective of future intensive negotiations within the framework established for the agriculture content of Agenda 2000 will be to achieve a balanced outcome.

The European Parliament has already received the report to the European Council in Cardiff on Agenda 2000 which will allow the honourable Member to have a more detailed overview of the progress thus achieved on Agenda 2000 and in particular on the milk quota regime.

To summarize the situation on the specific issue of a 2 % increase and its distribution, I would say that, at this stage, while several delegations could accept a rise in quota, the proposed method of distribution of the extra quota was controversial. Some delegations welcomed the priority allocation to certain categories of producers, and in particular those in mountain areas. Some delegations thought the quotas should be increased across the board for all Member States. Others, such as the honourable Member of Parliament, were of the opinion that if extra quota would be allocated to certain priority categories of producers, priority should also be given to groups other than young farmers or farmers in mountain areas, for example to farmers operating on marginal land or in otherwise disadvantaged areas.

The Council will, of course, in its future deliberations, take into due consideration the opinion to be delivered by the European Parliament.

Question No 41 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0596/98)

Subject: Breached dam in southern Spain

The devastating accident in Spain this spring, in which the dam retaining a waste reservoir at a mine belonging to the Swedish mining company Boliden Metall at Los Frailes was breached, is a direct consequence of taking too high a risk. The accident released huge quantities of pollutants, laying waste to extensive areas of countryside and farmland. SNF, the Swedish Society for Nature Conservation, is calling on the EU to do all it can to ensure that companies lessen the risks of their causing accidents, especially in areas of high environmental value. There should be greater openness with regard to the granting of authorization in the case of mining activity. Financial penalties should be imposed on countries which contravene EU environmental rules. Will the Council take steps to ensure that the actions of the parties concerned in connection with this accident are investigated? What steps is it planning to take to prevent similar accidents from happening in future?

Answer

The Council would like, first of all, to remind the honourable Member that it is the duty of the Commission to ensure that measures taken pursuant to the Treaty are applied.

The Council would then like to point out that, in order to prevent damage to the environment similar to the one described by the honourable Member, it took steps to strengthen relevant Community legislation as, for instance, Council Directive 97/11/EC amending Directive 85/337/EEC on the assessment of the effects of certain public and private projects on the environment and Council Directive 96/61/EC concerning integrated pollution prevention and control.

Although these two Directives are not going to be transposed by Member States before 1999, the Council is confident that their proper implementation by Member States will significantly contribute to avoiding accidents such as the one described in the honourable Member's question.

My Right Honourable Friend the Deputy Prime Minister raised the Donana disaster at the meeting of the Environment Council this morning. The Council had a useful discussion of the issue. They agreed that the Commission would examine what lessons could be learned from the disaster for the future.

Question No. 42 from Mr. Ioannis Theonas (H−0597/98)

Subject: Adverse repercussions of privatization in Greece.

The programme announced by the Greek government in the context of commitments which it undertook when the drachma entered the Exchange Rate Mechanism includes the privatization of a series of profitable companies such as the Ionian Bank, the Public Petroleum Corporation, the Capital Water and Sewage Company and the ports authorities and has provoked a storm of reaction and demonstrations by workers. In its proposal for Greece, which forms part of general economic guidelines, the Commission insists that the commitments undertaken by the Greek government be fulfilled as quickly as possible. This proposal, which is tantamount to direct intervention by the EU in the status of corporate ownership, even violates articles 222 and 90 of the EU Treaty.

Does the Council not feel that these procedures will help in the short term to reduce the government deficit to within the limits set out in the Maastrict Treaty but that, in the long term, they will have an adverse impact by depriving the wider public sector of an important source of income, which will disappear once these profitable companies pass into private hands?

Answer

On 5 June 1998 the Council formulated its draft for the broad guidelines of the economic policies of the Member States and of the Community.

In this draft, the Council underlined that Greece should continue its budgetary consolidation efforts, notably through the rigorous implementation of the measures announced by the government when the drachma joined the ERM. These measures include a further reduction in primary expenditure, extended privatization plans, the extensive rationalization of the public sector, and the reform of the social security system in the medium term.

The Council therefore does not share the concerns of the honourable Member.

Question No 43 by James Fitzsimons (H−0600/98)

Subject: Developing an EU Sports Policy taking into account amateur sports in countries such as Ireland

2. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 57 by Esko Seppänen (H−0515/98)

Subject: Freedom of competition and discounts for members of cooperatives

In many places in Finland, the local telephone networks are owned either by economic associations or by cooperatives. They may have hundreds of thousands of members. Membership is open to all. Members have adopted systems under which they obtain for themselves cheaper monthly subscriptions or call charges.

Some authorities in Finland claim that the Commission takes the view that telephone associations or cooperatives which are open to all are not permitted to grant their members discounts on subscriptions or call charges. Does the Commission insist, in the name of freedom of competition, that all economic activity must be carried out by public limited companies? What kind of discounts does the Commission consider that cooperatives may grant their members?

Answer

In its reply to Written Question No E−0836798 the Commission has already confirmed that it applies the same approach to all commercial organisations which are in dominant positions, without regard to the legal status under which they are incorporated. The concerns regarding Finland do not relate to the fact that local operators have chosen to operate voice telephony service as associations and cooperatives but to possible abuse of dominant positions.

In the past, the Commission has on different occasions, under the competition rules of the Treaty, reviewed the granting of cumulative rebates by dominant undertakings, which had the effect of preventing market entry by new competitors. The Court of Justice has confirmed in subsequent judgements that such rebates could constitute abuses of dominant position (e.g. judgment of 9 November 1983, in the Michelin case(1) ). Such behaviour could also infringe the competition rules if implemented by economic associations or by cooperatives if they were in dominant positions. The Court of First Instance confirmed that the Community competition rules apply to such organisations under the same conditions as to any other organisations (Case T−61789 Dansk pelsdyravlerforening). Consequently a dominant undertaking may not invoke its legal form to apply rebates restricting or preventing market entry by new competitors.

In line with the case‐law of the Court of Justice, the rebates combined with personal shares in cooperatives which aim or have as effect to bind the customer to use only this cooperative can be considered an abuse of a dominant position especially when leading to price discrimination. Such rebates would obviously discourage these subscribers to switch to potential competitors who cannot use rebates based on the return on investments made in the past when sheltered by monopoly rights, as the relevant cooperatives.

It should in any case by emphasised that these rules apply only to a company or cooperative which is in a dominant position according to Community law. The Commission has to assess the dominance and define the relevant market in each case before taking further measures against a company or cooperative.

Question No 58 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0525/98)

Subject: Classification of documents in government aid affair as secret

The Commission has decided to prohibit Swedish government transport aid to the Volvo trucks division in Umeå. The grounds for its decision are contained in a secret consultant report which is not even available to the parties directly involved. After the Commission refused to allow me to study the report, I contacted the European Ombudsman who asked to see the report on 28 April.

Will the Commission allow the EU Ombudsman to study the report? If so, does this imply that the report will also be made available to others, e.g. the parties concerned – Volvo and the Swedish Government?

Answer

As the honourable Member points out, the European Ombudsman requested on 28 April 1998 to conduct an examination at the Commission in connection with the complaint the honourable Member (together with his colleague Mr Wibe) has lodged with the European Ombudsman.

In this context, a meeting between the Commission and the Ombudsman's staff was arranged on 8 May 1998, where the representatives of the Ombudsman were given an opportunity to see and to read through the report. A copy of the report has also been submitted to the European Ombudsman's office.

The Commission does not, however, intend to disclose the report to the Swedish Government or to Volvo Truck Corporation, because it is an internal Commission document and was prepared to support the internal deliberations of the Commission. It does not therefore give a full account of the Commission's preliminary analysis and could thus lead to misinterpretations if released. The importance of the report should not be overemphasised, because by looking only at the report, and not all the relevant facts, the analysis of the case becomes incomplete and distorted.

The Commission therefore maintains that in order to protect the public interest, and more specifically its investigation into this infringement of Community competition law by Sweden, the document cannot be disclosed. It should be mentioned that this refusal is in line with the limits on the rights of access under the Commission's access policy.

Subject: Mergers in football clubs

Can the Commission inform Parliament of any discussions it may have had with UEFA, FIFA and FIPRO with a view to ensuring that Articles 85 and 86 of the Treaty are not breached by current proposals to amalgamate football clubs transnationally?

Question No 60 by Ulf Holm (H−0551/98)

Subject: Closure of Barsebäck 1

An appeal has been lodged by the company Sydkraft against the Swedish government's decision to close the Barsebäck 1 nuclear reactor, one of the grounds being that the decision breaches the principle of the irrevocability of beneficial administrative decisions which exists to safeguard legal certainty for the individual. Sydkraft believes that the strict view of retrospective legislation taken by EC law does not allow such action to the detriment of individuals. Since the authorization for Barsebäck was not subject to a time limit, Sydkraft believes that it is not possible to withdraw the authorization under EC law. However, Sydkraft does not provide further details of the sections of EC law to which it is referring in its claim.

Furthermore, Sydkraft believes that the Swedish government's decision to close Barsebäck 1 is at variance with EU competition rules as laid down in the Treaty of Rome. In addition to its submission to Sweden's Supreme Administrative Court, Sydkraft has therefore also notified the matter to the European Commission.

What legal grounds are there for Sydkraft's claim?

To what extent does the decision of Sweden's Supreme Administrative Court to close the reactor affect the Commission's handling of the matter?

When will the Commission have completed its consideration of this question?

Answer

As the Commission indicated in its reply to Written Question No E−0811/98, a complaint concerning the Swedish Government's decision to force the closure of the Barsebäck 1 nuclear reactor has been lodged with the Commission under the EC Treaty competition rules.

The complaint focuses only on the alleged anti‐competitive and discriminatory aspects of the Swedish Government's measures to reduce nuclear energy production in Sweden. It is not concerned with whether such reduction should occur at all.

The Commission is pursuing the case and is currently making fact‐finding. The case is being dealt with under the normal procedure of the Commission with the aim of completing the consideration of the case as soon as possible.

Question No 61 by Pat Gallagher (H−0579/98)

Subject: State aid to industry in Ireland

In light of recent statistics which show that the border county region and the West of Ireland have a standard of living below 75 % of the EU average, does the European Commission believe that state aid should continue to industry in these areas at a level of 40 %, particularly for the period after the year 2000?

Answer

When the Irish map of assisted areas was approved in 1988, the whole of the Member State had a GDP per capita lower than 75 % of the EC average and thus was eligible as an assisted area on the basis of Article 92 (3) (a) of the EC Treaty (’regions where the standard of living is abnormally low or where there is serious underemployment’). The aid intensity ceiling for such regions could reach 75 % of the investment costs. Indeed, the ceilings applying to the West of Ireland and to the border county region were fixed between 71.4 % and 75 % and will remain at these levels until the end of 1999.

The analysis for the application of Article 92 (3) (a) has always been conducted on the level of regions which are classified as NUTS II (in the nomenclature of Statistical Territorial Units). In the light of recent statistical data, the whole of Ireland, being a NUTS II region, will not benefit from the above provision after the end of 1999, since its GDP per capita is already substantially higher than 75 % of the Community average. Therefore, future regional aid in Ireland will have to be based on Article 92 (3) © of the EC Treaty (’aid to facilitate the development of certain economic areas...’).

The West of Ireland and the border county region do have a GDP per capita below 75 % of the Community average, but do not qualify as NUTS II level regions. Therefore the application of Article 92 (3) (a) is not possible for them after the end of 1999. Choosing ad hoc geographical units other than NUTS II to qualify for eligibility under the above Article, would undermine legal certainty and equality of treatment of the different Member States in the application of the method.

The Irish authorities will notify within the first quarter of 1999 the new map of assisted areas for the period from 01.01.2000 to 31.12.2006. In assessing the proposed map the Commission will apply the new guidelines on national regional aid adopted on 16.12.97, which allow for a gradual phasing‐out of the current aid intensity ceilings.

Question No 62 by Richard Howitt (H−0584/98)

Subject: New Holland (Basildon) and European competition rules

Given the European Court of Justice's recent decision against New Holland in my constituency, outlawing the UK Agricultural Tractor Agreement, what justification does the European Commission have for keeping secret information about tractor sales in the UK which is freely available through national government authorities in Italy, Spain, France and Germany? Is not fair competition better served by making market information freely available in every Member State?

Answer

The honourable Member will find the reasons for the Commission's attitude towards the information exchange in question in the text of the Decision, and in particular at points 37, 43 and 49. That Decision bears the number 92/157/EEC and was published on 13 March 1992 in Official Journal No L−68, on page 19.

That Decision was addressed to the Agricultural Engineers Association and to the eight members of the exchange. Two companies applied to the Court of First Instance to have the Decision annulled. The application was rejected on 27 October 1994. The two thereupon appealed to the Court of Justice, which in turn rejected that appeal on 28 May 1998. Since the two principal judicial authorities in the Community have thus entirely confirmed the legality of the Decision in question, the Commission feels it would not be proper to add any comments to the text of that Decision. The Decision remains, for the time being, the only formal instrument for assessment of information exchanges of this type.

Question No 63 by Alfred Lomas (H−0496/98)

Subject: Harmonisation of qualifying age for male and female pensioners for free travel passes

Following the EC ruling that free medical prescriptions in Britain have to be paid to men and women at the same age, bringing men in line with women by reducing the male age from 65 years old to 60 years old, does the Commission agree that this should also apply to the issue of pensioners' free travel passes?

Answer

The EC Directive on equal treatment cannot be applied to the issue of pensioners' free travel passes. According to the case law of the Court of Justice, and more particularly its judgment of 11 July 1996, in Case C−228/94 (Atkins) this matter falls outside the scope of Directive 79/7/EEC of 19 December 1978 on progressive implementation of the principle of equal treatment for women and men in matters of social security.

Question No 64 by Mark Watts (H−0505/98)

Subject: Pollution and premature death

A UK Government report has recently found that between 12 000 and 24 000 people per year die prematurely from the effects of pollution, most of which is caused by fumes from cars and lorries.

How many people per year die prematurely in the EU as a whole as a result of pollution?

Is the Commission proposing to take action to reduce this figure? If so, what are the targets, and how will they be achieved?

Answer

The Report of the Committee on the Medical Effects of Air Pollution on Health in the United Kingdom estimates that 12 000 to 24 000 deaths are brought forward each year in urban areas in Great Britain owing to daily fluctuations in concentrations of sulphur dioxide, particulate matter and ozone. It is not possible to extrapolate these estimates to the Community as a whole, owing to lack of data on levels of air pollution and exposure. But the Commission's proposal for a Directive on limit values for sulphur dioxide, oxides of nitrogen, particulate matter and lead in ambient air(2) aims explicitly at reducing the impact of pollution on mortality, on human health and on the environment. The Commission is also preparing a proposal for air quality standards for ozone, and a strategy for reducing precursors of ozone, with the same aims in mind.

The proposals are part of a legislative programme to tackle air pollution from transport and all other important sources. Emissions of sulphur dioxide will be greatly reduced by implementation of Directive 96/61/EC concerning integrated pollution prevention and control(3) . The Commission is also preparing Revision of the Large Combustion Plants Directive(4) . Both instruments will reduce primary emissions of particles from industry and emissions of the precursors of secondary particulate matter and ozone. Where transport is concerned, the proposed new emission limit values for motor vehicles and fuel quality standards(5) , which will take effect in the year 2000, are designed to meet ambitious targets for particulate matter, ozone and other pollutants. More stringent limit values will probably come into force in 2005.

The legislative programme is being supported by research on the effects of air pollution and on ways of reducing it further. As the UK report points out, it looks only at the effects of daily fluctuations in air pollution and these are not fully quantified. Those most likely to be affected are patients with severe pre‐existing disease. No estimate was made of the effects of long‐term exposure to current levels of air pollution. Further research in 32 European cities should allow more precise estimates of the impact of air pollution within the next few years. Research is also underway on the early and long‐term effects of exposure to benzene and diesel emissions and on cleaner and alternative fuels and alternative propulsion systems.

Finally, to complement research activities and regulatory proposals on air pollutants, the Commission proposed in 1997 public health community action on the prevention of pollution‐related diseases (COM(97)266. It intends to define action for exposed individuals in order to prevent or mitigate health effects linked to environmental pollution, in particular, air pollution.

Question No 65 by Ludivina García Arias (H−0506/98)

In connection with the serious situation created in the Coto de Doñana by the spillage of untreated sludge produced by a Swedish mining company, with disastrous effects for both the environment and the economic life of the local population, what steps does the Commission intend to take to ensure that European legislation and programmes connected with mining take serious account of the need for a specific and preventive solution for treating waste produced by mining work, in view of the characteristics and volume of such waste?

What steps does it intend to take to ensure that adequate funds and resources are devoted to developing effective methods of treating this waste and to providing the relevant authorities with appropriate mechanisms to guarantee treatment and prevent accidents in future?

Question No 71 by Paul Lannoye (H−0535/98)

Subject: Ecological damage to the Doñana region

Question No 72 by Doeke Eisma (H−0536/98)

Subject: Chemical spill from the Aspirsa mine in Spain

Has the Commission now received full information on the chemical content of the toxic mixture emanating from the incident at the Aspirsa mine at Aznalcollar near Seville in Spain?

Joint Answer

When news of the disaster emerged, the Commission contacted the Spanish authorities and began exchanging information relative to the measures taken to protect the national park and its surroundings, as well as minimising the consequences of the accident. The Commission offered to send a team experts of the Community task force to cooperate with the Spanish authorities in evaluating the situation and measures undertaken to alleviate the problem.

During an information meeting held in Brussels on 12 May 1998, the Director‐General of Nature Conservation and the President of Spain's National Centre for Scientific Research informed the Commission of the steps taken to limit and repair the damage caused by the accident at the Aznacollar Mine. Supplementary information has since been sent to the Commission concerning the measures applied to treat the contaminated waters and collect the toxic material.

The Commission is still in the process of collecting information relating to the chemical spill and contacts with the Spanish authorities have been organized to this end. At this stage and on the basis of the information available, the Commission is not in a position to decide on concrete proposals or measures concerning the safety of toxic waste containers similar to the one used by the plant in Aznacollar.

The Commission is required to monitor the application of measures taken by the institutions to implement the provisions of the EC Treaty. However, it is the responsibility of the competent national authorities to ensure that national, regional and local legislation on waste is properly applied.

Directive 75/442/EEC of 15 July 1975 on waste excludes from its field of application waste resulting from the prospecting, extraction, processing and storage of mineral resources, as well as the exploitation of quarries. The directive entered into force in 1977 and was transposed into Spanish law by Law 42/1975 relative to urban solid waste. This law foresaw that Law 22/1973 relative to mines, applied in the case of mining waste.

Directive 75/442/EEC was modified by Directive 92/156/EEC. Article 2 of the modified directive established that waste resulting from prospecting, extraction, treatment and storage of mineral resources, plus the working of quarries, would be excluded from the scope of the directive, where they are already covered by other legislation. The Commission understands the term ‘other legislation’ to apply to Community legislation. Specific rules for particular instances, or supplementing those of Directive 75/442/EEC on the management of particular categories of waste may be laid down by means of individual directives.

At present, there is no specific Community legislation on this type of waste. Therefore, Directive 75/442/EEC, as modified by Directive 91/156/EEC, has applied to this type of waste since 1 April 1993, the date by which it was to have been transposed into national legislation.

The Commission brought a case against Spain at the Court of Justice for non‐transposition of Directive 91/156/EEC by the proscribed deadline. In its decision of 5 June 1997, the Court of Justice found that Spain had failed to comply with its obligations under Directive 91/156/EEC. The Spanish authorities have now informed the Commission that the Directive was transposed by Law 10/1998 of 21 April 1998, on waste.

Article 4 of the Directive establishes that the competent national authorities shall take the necessary measures to ensure that waste is recovered or disposed of without endangering human health and without using processes or methods which could harm the environment. Similarly, Article 8 places a responsibility on the competent national authorities to ensure that the holder of any waste has it recovered and disposed of in accordance with the provisions of the Directive.

Temporary storage of waste is not defined in Directive 75/442/EEC. However, this is also covered by Article 4, as mentioned above. The Commission Proposal for a directive on Landfill of Waste defines the temporary deposit of waste to be a deposit lasting less than one year. Hereby any storage of waste which lasts for more than one year would in future need to comply with the provisions of the proposal, which seeks to ensure environmentally safe and controlled land filling of wastes.

With regard to the question of long‐term monitoring, existing Community legislation on water protection provides only for a limited number of water bodies to be protected, i.e. water for drinking water abstraction, bathing waters, fish waters and shellfish waters.

The proposal for a Water Framework directive, currently undergoing its first reading by the European Parliament, aims to expand the scope of water protection to all waters, surface waters and ground waters, and set the obligation to achieve and/or maintain ‘good status’ for all waters within a certain deadline. Comprehensive measuring and monitoring will be one of the main pillars of the Directive. The setting of quality objectives and the monitoring will, for surface waters, consist of biological elements, hydro morphological elements as well as chemical and physicochemical elements supporting the biological elements.

Question No 66 by Carmen Díez de Rivera Icaza (H−0530/98)

Subject: EU checks on the operations of Aspirsa‐Boliden in Spain

Has the mining company responsible for dumping toxic chemicals in Aznalcóllar (Seville, Spain) received any direct or indirect subsidy from the Union?

Answer

The European Regional Development Fund co‐financed a project in the Aznalcóllar mine as part of the 1994‐1999 Incentivos Regionales operational programme. In this respect it is useful to note that the choice of projects to be subsidized through this operational programme is the responsibility of the relevant Spanish authorities. The Commission, which sits on the Monitoring Committee, receives information on each chosen project after selection by the said authorities.

According to information received from the Spanish authorities, an amount of approximately ECU 9.62 million was paid out, compared with total co‐funding of around ECU 20.55 million. These authorities have now suspended payment of the remaining funds.

Question No 73 by Per Gahrton (H−0507/98)

Subject: Risks associated with children's vaccinations

Some researchers claim that the benefits of vaccinating children may sometimes be outweighed by the health hazards posed by certain chemical components of the vaccine and by 'overprotecting' children so that they do not develop immunity naturally. Some of the hazardous substances that may form part of the vaccine include aluminium, formaldehyde and even mercury. In February 1998, The Lancet published details of a possible link between the vaccine against measles, mumps and rubella and autism. In Sweden, a number of current cases have triggered a debate on the subject, including detailed coverage by Svenska Dagbladet on 5 May 1998.

Is the Commission aware of the risks associated with certain children's vaccinations? Does the Commission intend to take any measures that are within its powers to reduce the risks that may be associated with children's vaccinations?

Answer

Vaccination is the most powerful preventive measure against infectious diseases. According to the World Health Organisation (WHO), the development of new vaccines for diseases like rotavirus diarrhoea and presently pneumococcal pneumonia in children and the effective wide‐spread deployment of under‐used vaccines for diseases like measles can save up to 12 million lives a year.

Vaccines are drugs and as such are subject to marketing authorizations (MAs), issued according to the measures set out in Council Directives 65/65/EEC and 75/319/EEC, following a scientific evaluation based on the application of an essential principle in the field of vaccination, that is, the balance between the advantages in protecting an individual"s health and the possible risks of a deterioration in the wellbeing of healthy individuals to whom the vaccine is administered.

Under the terms of the measures of Chapter 5b of Council Directive 75/319/EEC relating to pharmacovigilance, Member States which have authorized vaccines to be put on the market, including those aimed at children, are responsible for implementing vaccine control measures which include, amongst other things, notification of any undesirable effects. This may, moreover, be a condition of the granting of an MA and, if need be, for the modification, suspension or withdrawal of a vaccine"s MA, all of which the European Agency for the Evaluation of Medicinal Products must be kept informed about.

The prevailing scientific opinion is that there is no evidence which warrants the abandonment of the combined vaccine against measles, mumps and rubella, given its benefits. This opinion has been put forward in authoritative scientific publications, including ‘The Lancet’.

Question No 74 by Paul Rübig (H−0509/98)

Subject: Evaluation of budget headings with a positive effect on employment

At least since the Luxembourg summit in November 1997, employment policy has become one of the main concerns of our European policy. It is well known that the annual budgetary procedure can help to stimulate the labour market in a number of ways.

In my opinion it is essential to consider the EU budget too in terms of its effect on employment policy. Can the Commissioner give me any information on the assessment of the relevant budget headings with effects on the employment situation and their development in recent years?

Answer

The European Commission adopted a Communication on Community policies in support of employment on 3 June. In it the Commission mentions several Community policies and actions aimed at supporting job creation, Structural and Cohesion Funds, the 5th Research and Development Framework Programme, the Community Action Programmes for Education, Training and Youth, the Trans‐European Networks and the Employment and Growth Initiative.

The Commission does not produce overall assessments of the effects of Community expenditure on employment. However, evaluations are undertaken for each of the main Community programmes and the identification of the employment impact on Community interventions is an important component of these evaluations. For example, the Commission is currently finalizing the mid‐term evaluation of the programmes carried out with the resources of the Structural Funds.

Following these mid‐term evaluations, many of the programmes have been modified to enhance their impact on employment, and emphasize a number of key priorities. These include the need to shift from passive to active labour market measures, strengthening links between education systems and the labour market, the potential for job creation through local development and employment initiatives as in particular territorial employment pacts; and, maximising the spill‐over effect on local employment from infra structural work.

Question No 75 by Kirsi Piha (H−0510/98)

Subject: The northern dimension and the EU budget

At the Luxembourg summit, the northern dimension was included on the agenda at Finland's initiative. The concept of the northern dimension covers cooperation in the areas of the environment, security and border regions, as well as the various sectors of energy cooperation.

The TACIS programme will be coming up for renewal in 1999. How does the Commission react to the idea of opening, in 1999, a separate budget heading for the northern dimension, thereby bringing together appropriations allocated to this region which formerly came from different sectors? In this way the northern dimension could become a more clearly manageable unit and would acquire a legitimate status through the EU budget.

Answer

The preparations for a new TACIS Regulation is currently underway within the Commission. The aim is to have a new regulation into place in the beginning of the year 2000.

Evaluations of the present programme have been carried out and meetings with interested parties such as the partner countries, and the business community equally are part of the preparatory process. It is in this context that consideration will be given to the future role TACIS can play in the realisation of the Northern Dimension aspects as indicated by the Honourable Member.

At the same time the attention of the Honourable Member is drawn to the fact that the Commission will present an interim report on the Northern Dimension prior to the Vienna European Council. This report will provide details on the role of TACIS in the region.

Question No 76 by Jaime Valdivielso de Cué (H−0513/98)

Subject: State aid

Could the Commission explain what criteria were used to estimate the sum of 40 000 million pesetas which it says the Spanish firm Hunosa received illegally?
