On 3 June 1998, after analysing these subsidies the Commission took a decision, which will also be published in the Official Journal.

Question No 77 by Nikitas Kaklamanis (H−0516/98)

Subject: Strengthening of Turkish occupying forces in Cyprus

According to reports, a reorganization of the 11th corps of the occupying army in Cyprus is well under way, with heavy weaponry being brought into the occupied sector. Specifically, in the first two months of 1998 alone, 43 'NUROL' armoured personnel carriers were transported to occupied Cyprus from Turkey, together with a large number of armoured recovery vehicles.

Altogether, 79 modern armoured personnel carriers, 51 'NUROL' personnel carriers and 16 self‐propelled guns have been transferred to the area in recent months, while many units of the occupying army have been converted into mobile brigades and motorized battalions.

What is the Commission's official position on this development, which poses a clear threat of destabilization over a wide area and shows that Turkey has no desire to settle the Cyprus question but is instead continuing its usual strategy of operating by fait accompli in Cyprus?

Answer

The Commission reiterates its support for the UN's efforts to find a comprehensive settlement in Cyprus on the basis of the relevant UN resolutions and High Level Agreements. The Commission would like to see the two Cypriot communities resuming direct high‐level talks in the UN framework running in parallel with the negotiations for accession to the Community. The Commission's preferred objective would be the accession of a bi‐communal, bi‐zonal federation of Cyprus. The Commission would like to urge all parties concerned to work constructively towards this goal and to refrain from introducing new weaponry onto the island, which risks raising tensions in the whole region.

Question No 78 by Luciano Vecchi (H−0517/98)

Subject: Plan to exploit oil deposits in Chad and Cameroon

The World Bank is getting ready to finance a plan to exploit oil deposits in southern Chad and construct an oil pipeline some 1100 km long through Cameroon on behalf of a consortium formed by Esso, Shell and Elf Aquitaine. Three hundred new wells will be opened, and the plan includes the construction of pumping infrastructures and a floating terminal in the Atlantic Ocean off the coast of Cameroon.

When presented the plan prompted loud protests from NGOs in the World Bank for the following reasons: failure to consult local communities; the environmental impact of the plan; constant violations of human rights in Chad and the use by the World Bank of IDA (International Development Assistance) funds earmarked for poverty relief to fund the project.

Is the Commission aware of the plan? What is its opinion on the subject? Does it not think it should intervene vis‐à‐vis the World Bank and the Member States of the Union and call for a socio‐environmental impact assessment of the plan and ask the World Bank to provide further information regarding the use of IDA funds, to which the Member States of the Union are among the major contributors?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the plan to exploit oil deposits in the Doba region of southern Chad and of the construction of an oil pipeline to relay the production of these oil deposits out via the Cameroon port of Kribi.

Chad is one of the poorest countries in the world and the Commission considers it has the right to develop one of its rare natural resources, which is likely to generate substantial income for the national authorities. It thus recommends a positive approach, through political dialogue with the Chad authorities which should cover:

‐ the measures to be taken in order to protect the environment, as well as the health of those affected by the oil exploitation;

‐ the allocation of a reasonable share of the oil income to support the village communities within the oil producing region and for the protection of the legitimate rights of these communities;

‐ in general, the effective and efficient use of oil income in the fight against poverty.

Without directly being involved in the oil project, the Commission is willing to enter into such a dialogue with the other major partners of Chad.

With regard to the use of IDA funds initially envisaged by the World Bank, most recent information indicates that they may now resort to other means of funding for this project.

Question No 79 by Mary Banotti (H−0522/98)

Subject: EU/Israel Trade Accord

Can the Commission explain what steps it is taking to ensure that products manufactured in Jewish settlements in the West Bank and Gaza are not gaining preferential access to the EU under the EU/Israel trade accord?

Is it not the case that products manufactured in settlements in the West Bank and Gaza should not be considered as manufactured in Israel?

Answer

The Commission approved, beginning of May 1998, two communications to the Council on ‘Euro‐Mediterranean Cumulation of Origin’ and ‘Implementation of the Interim Agreement on Trade and Trade‐related Matters between the European Community and Israel’.

The latter stresses, among other issues, the fact that preferential access to Community markets for exports originating in Israeli settlements contravenes agreed rules of origin since these territories do not form part of the State of Israel under public international law.

Since there are indications that exports from Israeli settlements in the West Bank and Gaza Strip under preferential treatment granted by the interim agreement between the Community and Israel are taking place, the Commission will verify the accuracy of this information. Should it be confirmed, the Commission recommends to bring to an end such violations.

This action does not represent a sanction against Israel but demands correct implementation of the existing agreement freely entered by the parties. Limiting the scope of territorial application of the Interim agreement to the 1967's borders according to international public law, does not pre‐judge final status negotiations between Israelis and Palestinians.

Question No 80 by José Barros Moura (H−0527/98)

Subject: Information leak on the evaluation of the national employment plans

Can the Commission explain the circumstances surrounding the distribution of a document containing a technical evaluation of the national employment plans, including criticisms of (inter alia) Italy, the UK, Belgium and Portugal, despite the fact that at the time of distribution it was already known that this document had not been approved by the Commissioners?

While the Commission has already recognized that this was a 'regrettable leak' of what was simply an internal document, is it now in a position to deny all and any political responsibility for this 'leak'?

What action has the Commission taken, or what action does it intend to take, to identify the reasons and those responsible? Or does it consider that no action is necessary?

What is the Commission's assessment of the impact of such events on public opinion nationally and in the EU? Does it consider such events to advance or hinder public support for the EU, of whose interests the Commission is the guardian?

Answer

On 13 May the Commission adopted a communication(6) with the aim of studying the commitments undertaken by Member States within their national action plans for employment and of assessing whether these commitments corresponded to the content and the objectives of the 1998 employment guidelines. It was also intended to serve as a basis for discussion at the Cardiff European Council with regard to current progress.

The public presentation of this communication was accompanied by the distribution of the Commission department document(7) to which the honourable Member is referring. This document was never intended for approval by the Commissioners, its function being to present the results of a detailed examination of the national plans submitted by Member States following the decisions of the Luxembourg Summit, results which were essentially taken up in the communication.

The document is a thematic synthesis of the national plans according to each of the 19 guidelines. As in any exercise of this kind, it makes explicit reference to particular States in order to illustrate its conclusions without, however, undertaking a critical analysis of every national plan, which the Commission does not consider to be appropriate at this stage.

Given the public nature of the Commission department document, it is not correct to describe its distribution as an information “leak’.

Question No 81 by John Cushnahan (H−0529/98)

Subject: Developing nations debt

Given the failure of the G8 Summit which met in Birmingham recently to adopt the necessary measures to alleviate the huge debt problems faced by developing nations will the Commission take an initiative on this issue?

Answer

The Commission does not consider it appropriate to speak of the ‘failure’ of the G8 Summit to adopt measures to alleviate developing countries debt problems. The final communiqué of the G8 explicitly supports the speedy and determined extension of debt relief to more countries, within the terms of the HIPC initiative. Moreover, the G8 also agreed on the need to consider ways for debt relief mechanisms, including the HIPC initiative where appropriate, to be used to release more and earlier resources for essential rehabilitation, particularly for those countries with arrears to the IMF.

In this respect, it should also be underlined that solutions to the debt problem of the poorest countries require concerted action among all creditors in order to have the necessary impact, as it is the case for the HIPC initiative.

For its part, the European Community took early action to redress the debt problem of the poor countries. As early as 1990 it decided to channel the major part of its development assistance in the form of grants to alleviate the burden that these countries were facing. This explains why the Community is today primarily a donor of grant resources, why it warmly welcomed the debt initiative for HIPC implementing economic reforms, and why it decided to fully participate in it.

The Commission also took the initiative of adapting its assistance programmes to countries benefiting from the HIPC initiative so as to increase its support to them and enhance social programmes in them.

In addition, the Council, on proposal from the Commission, adopted 12 February a decision allowing the Community to participate in the HIPC initiative by providing exceptional assistance that would help the eligible countries with reducing their debt towards the Community.

A first amount of ECU 40 million, additional to normal EDF resources, has been set aside for this purpose.

Question No 82 by Kirsten Jensen (H−0539/98)

Subject: Death of fish in the North Sea

Will the Commission explain how the Japanese alga, chatonella, has ended up in Danish waters, and say whether its appearance may be due to shipping in the region, e.g. discharges from ballast tanks and transfer on ships' bottom paint, or to climate change?

Will it also state whether the Member States exchange information on chatonella?

Is it planning to take up this issue at the Oceans Conference due to be held in Portugal in June?

Answer

Chatonella algae are from the rhaphidophyceae family. There are only twenty to thirty known species. They are most commonly found in Japan and Asia where they are found in big blooms and cause fish deaths. Chatonella is an algae that creates toxins, but so far, there is no indication that they create toxins in large amounts in Nordic waters.

They were first found off Norway (Skagerrak), where they killed some fish in salmon nurseries. They are now also found along the western coast of Jutland and have also caused some fish deaths there, in particular ‘Belena’ fish. In spring 1998 there were some high outflows of nutrients from German rivers. This caused high nutrient levels along the Danish coast which influences the growth of this algae. They are not, however, particularly linked with high nutrient inputs or eutrophication.

Chatonella is suspected to have been present for quite a while in the North Sea. The exact species is not yet know, but if it is turns out to be the Japanese species, then it is likely to have come into the North Sea via the cleaning of ballast tanks of vessels having taken ballast water off Asia.

More information can also be found on the homepage of the Marine Ecology Department: www.dmu.dk in Danish.

The Commission is not aware that this issue is going to be discussed at the conferences scheduled in Portugal.

Question No 83 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−0543/98)

Subject: Commission obligation to monitor the use of Community funds to prevent them being used for Government propaganda

Given that the Greek Government is using substantive amounts of Community funding – for instance, Drs. 1.5 billion from the CSF for Greece – for Government propaganda and television broadcasts, without respecting the balance of political power in Greece and without giving MEPs the opportunity to inform Greek public opinion, will the Commission say whether it considers that the relevant procedures governing advertising campaigns have been respected as regards publicity for the regional operational programmes and whether it can assure Parliament that it was aware of – and had agreed to – the actions taken by the Greek regional authorities?

Answer

In accordance with current Community regulations, Member States ensure that actions which receive assistance from Community Structural Funds are given adequate publicity so that potential beneficiaries and professional organizations may be made more aware of the opportunities offered by these actions, and public opinion made more aware of the role played by the Community in this process.

The “Technical assistance’ operational programme of the Community support framework (CSF) for Greece provides for the realization of an action plan in the field of publicity and information, with a budget of ECU 6.2 million. The national and regional OPs and Community initiatives also include publicity and information campaigns with smaller budgets.

In accordance with the principle of subsidiarity, the Greek authorities are responsible for the correct implementation of the procedure for invitation to tender and the bids for publicity and information contracts for the CSF and the different regional and national programmes. The Commission was consulted regarding the terms of reference of these tenders before they were opened.

The appropriate Greek ministry consulted the Commission with regard to the action plan, as well as the national “Publicity and Information’ guide for the use of the chairmen of the monitoring committees of the operational programmes and Community initiatives so that a coherent approach would be achieved. The overall actions decided by the Greek authorities in the area of publicity and information are, moreover, in keeping with the rules laid down in the Community guide on the subject. Precise actions are discussed with the Commission before being initiated.

As far as the Commission is aware, the TV, radio and press advertising is politically neutral. Furthermore since its launch in 1997, the neutrality of the action plan has not been criticized.

With regard to the talk‐shows which were organized in 1997, the participation of MEPs from the major political groups was assured on many occasions. The Commission will ask the relevant national authorities to make sure that this kind of balanced participation is always guaranteed in the future.

Given that there will be local elections in Greece in October 1998, it has been agreed with the Greek government that the publicity and information campaign co‐financed by the Structural Funds will be suspended from August until October, in order to avoid any criticism.

Question No 84 by Sirkka‐Liisa Anttila (H−0545/98)

Subject: New forestry subsidies proposed under Agenda 2000 violate GATT rules

In the Commission's Agenda 2000 proposal of 18 March 1998, it is proposed that EU subsidies should increasingly be extended to the forestry sector. Such new forestry subsidies must not lead to a common forestry policy whereby forestry is as thoroughly directed, regulated and subsidized as agriculture. Forestry subsidies should be strictly confined to areas where no other economic activity is possible.

It is likely that strong pressure will be brought to bear during the forthcoming WTO negotiations in favour of free trade, and there will be calls, inter alia, for cuts in farm subsidies. Hitherto, forestry has been a sector where pure free trade and market economics prevailed, but once Agenda 2000 has been implemented, forestry too will be subsidized. It is vital therefore to ascertain in advance whether the forestry subsidies proposed under Agenda 2000 are compatible with GATT rules. Forestry subsidies must not cause fresh pressures in the WTO negotiations, as we are already facing considerable problems in agriculture.

Are the forestry subsidies proposed under Agenda 2000 contrary to GATT rules and if so, how will the Commission solve the problem?

Answer

The draft Regulation on rural development which was proposed following Agenda 2000 in no way anticipates market support subsidies for the forestry sector along the lines of those which the Regulation on the markets for agricultural products provides for. In fact, the draft Regulation is limited to continuing the forestry measures currently in existence, whether as part of the CAP accompaniment measures or as part of rural development in the Objective 1, 5b and 6 zones. The Regulation anticipates that these measures may be the focus of all rural development programmes. The only real innovation is the aid provided for in Article 30 of the proposal, which is limited to forests whose environmental balance is in danger. Furthermore, it is appropriate to underline that the proposal does not aim to introduce a system of subsidies but to co‐finance programmes formulated by Member States, which may also include forestry measures.

With regard to the WTO rules, it must be noted that the measures are not considered as agricultural support measures and consequently are not subject to the subsidy reductions in the agricultural sector. The granting of financial subsidies is not in contradiction with the WTO rules and these subsidies should not have any detrimental effect on the interests of the other members of the WTO.

The integrated approach of rural development policy may cover a number of sectors and encompass intersectoral actions affecting, for example, agriculture, tourism, the environment or forestry. On the other hand, the Commission in no way anticipates the drawing up of a common forestry policy similar to the CAP.

Question No 85 by Brian Simpson (H−0547/98)

Subject: Postal services

In view of the fact that many points do not appear to be running smoothly on the subject of postal services, can the Commission indicate when it will be reporting back to Parliament on the issue of terminal dues and when will the Commission approve the REIMS II agreement?

Answer

Action taken on the European Parliament's resolution

On Wednesday, 17 June the Commission is to examine, with a view to its adoption, a document setting out the action which it intends to take on the European Parliament's resolution of 19 December 1997 on terminal dues. The official forwarding of this document could follow at the end of the month.

Amongst the various measures envisaged, the Commission plans to conduct an inquiry on present remailing practices in order to get a better picture of the recent development of the problems connected with remailing and the elements at stake. The Commission will then inform the European Parliament of the results of this inquiry.

The REIMS II agreement

The REIMS II agreement, which is intended as a substitute for the REIMS I agreement previously notified in December 1995, was notified to the Commission on 31 October 1997. The REIMS I agreement was not complete when notified, and the Commission had informed the operators of the additional information required and the need to improve the agreement in order to obtain extra benefits for private and professional users.

However, the operators have negotiated this new agreement, REIMS II, amongst themselves, and it does indeed present further problems. The difference between REIMS I and REIMS II is essentially a more rapid increase in terminal dues with a reduced spin‐off for the improvement of the quality of the service. Article 85(3) of the Treaty does not allow the Commission to take a positive decision on an agreement unless that agreement allows consumers a fair share of the resulting benefit.

It is therefore the responsibility of the postal operators to give a satisfactory reply as soon as possible to the concerns already expressed by the Commission in January 1998.

It is therefore impossible to fix a precise date for a Commission decision on a possible exemption.

Question No 86 by Inger Schörling (H−0552/98)

Subject: Construction of motorway in Poland under the TEN

Through the European Investment Bank and the PHARE programme, the Union is funding the construction of a motorway through the Mount St Anna's Landscape Park in Poland. There are five nature reserves within the park, which is protected by Polish environment laws, but the regional authorities responsible for the area have now decided that a strip of land in the centre of the park, where the plans show that the motorway is to be built, is no longer to form part of the landscape park. This method of circumventing regulations has been criticized by Polish environment organizations, among others, both on environmental and legal grounds. The motorway, which forms part of the EU's trans‐European networks project, will severely affect the Mount St Anna's Landscape Park.

How does the Commission justify EU participation in the funding of a motorway to be built straight through an otherwise protected area? Has the Commission carried out a complete investigation of the environmental impact and of possible alternative routes?

Answer

The Polish authorities have established a motorway construction programme, which includes the section of the A4 motorway to which the Honourable Member refers. The whole programme and the A4 motorway itself were the subject of environmental impact assessments. It is the Commission's understanding that all the analyses carried out by the Polish authorities fully respected the relevant international conventions signed by Poland. Moreover, the proposed route of the A4 motorway and alternative routes were the subject of consultations with local communities.

The Community agrees to Poland's request to co‐finance the construction of the A4 motorway through the Phare programme. This project fits in with the Community's TENs priorities, as it is part of the Crete corridor N°III, and meets the relevant criteria for EU involvement, namely additionality, readiness for implementation and IFI (EIB) participation. The Commission has already supported pre‐feasibility and feasibility studies and is now providing financial support for the construction of motorway bridges.

This project is a key element in the restructuring of Poland's transport system, needed to meet the challenges of economic modernisation in Poland and its neighbours.

With regard to questions 1 (the Commission defence of its financing of this motorway):

The Commission has accepted to co‐finance the A4 motorway construction project through a grant from the Phare programme because this project is a part of the Crete corridor III and meets the following CEC criteria of selection for investment: (1) implementation readiness, (2) additionality, (3) conditionality of IFI's involvement (EIB). The project was proven technically and economically feasible. Preliminary design, cost estimations and environmental impact studies have been completed. The modernisation of this corridor is considered as a priority by the Polish government in its motorway construction programme for which financial support from the European Investment Bank is scheduled as well. Through this co‐financing Phare will support the Polish government in the process of restructuring the transport system to meet the changing needs of the economy and to accommodate Poland's growing strategic role as a bridge between Western and Eastern European Countries. Moreover, significant benefits can be expected once the whole project has been implemented.

With regard to question 2 (Commission EIA and alternatives):

An environmental impact assessment was performed by Polish experts and accepted by the Ministry of the Environment, the Ministry of Home Affairs and Administration and the Ministry of Culture. All analyses presented in the report were conducted with full respect to the relevant international conventions signed by the Polish government. The selected solution includes all adjustments required by law and detailed evaluation of impact on the environment, which were the basis for obtaining the construction permits.

Alternative routes of the motorway in the area of St Ann Landscape Park, allowing for the selection of the currently implemented variant, have been the subject of consultations with the inhabitants of local communities at the beginning of the nineties.

Question No 87 by Alex Smith (H−0555/98)

Subject: Emergency inspections at Dounreay

Over the past two months the nuclear waste and reprocessing complex at Dounreay in Caithness, on Scotland's northern coast, has suffered a series of safety problems, including the severing of both primary and secondary electricity supply cables which were channelled through the same duct by a mechanical excavator, leaving the plant vulnerable to consequent accidents. The UK nuclear safety regulators have now decided to shut the operational sections of the plant, and have refused to allow the plant to accept any further imports of radioactive materials whilst the safety licence for the complex is under review. In light of earlier safety concerns expressed by the Commission over the Dounreay site, what emergency inspections have been ordered under Articles 35‐37 of the Euratom Treaty?

Answer

As stated in the question, the safety licence for the Dounreay complex is now under review by the competent national authorities. When that review is completed urgent consideration will be given to a further verification visit to Dounreay under the terms of Article 35 of the Euratom Treaty. In addition, whenever the clean‐up operations or the eventual decommissioning of the Dounreay site give rise to the disposal of new types of waste, general data on these operations will need to be submitted under the terms of Article 37 of the Euratom Treaty.

Question No 88 by Anne McIntosh (H−0557/98)

Subject: Tax differentials

When will the Commission draft a report to review the tax differentials on duty on wines, beers and spirits throughout the EU?

Answer

Firstly, the reality is that in the field of alcoholic drinks taxation there is no consensus among Member States as regards the most basic tax issues confronting the Community. As the same time, few would regard the current Community system of taxation in this field as being commensurate with the needs of an internal market, so the position should be improved.

Bearing that in mind, there is an attempt to identify a line of positive action that would be likely to obtain the necessary unanimous agreement of the Council. Nor should it be forgotten that, over the years, Parliament itself has been more or less equally divided on the issues involved. The situation was similar at the time of the Commission's last report on the minimum rates for alcoholic drinks. At that time the problems of the wine market, and the Commission's proposals to deal with them, were of particular concern. On that occasion the Commission decided not to make any proposals for further harmonization and both Council and Parliament generally welcomed that approach. The Commission is now looking again into the question and does not anticipate any great delay in producing its report.

Question No 89 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0558/98)

According to a ruling by the copyright clearing house Copyswede, non‐Swedish nationals are being prevented from subscribing to SVT Europa, a new Swedish (presumably) international channel that started transmitting on 10 December 1997. Question No 90 by Nuala Ahern (H−0559/98)

Subject: Greenpeace disclosures of high levels of radioactive contamination in sediment taken from the sea bed at the end of Sellafield's nuclear waste discharge pipe

On 27 May 1998, scientific results released by Greenpeace revealed that sediment taken from the sea bed at the end of Sellafield's nuclear waste discharge pipe contained:

Americium 24113 259 Bq kg (dry weight)
Caesium137372 152 Bq kg (dry weight)
Caesium134287 Bq kg (dry weight)
Cobalt 60489 Bq kg (dry weight)

Are these figures above the levels set by EU Basic Safety Standards radioactive materials? If such levels are above EU exemption levels, BNFL is required to report the existence of the radioactive materials. What measures will the Commission take to investigate BNFL's secret operations within EU legislation? How will the Commission seek to improve the regulation of nuclear power plants and eliminate the dangers posed by the production of high‐level nuclear waste during reprocessing? Will the Commission specify the action it will take in the light of the Greenpeace disclosures?

Answer

The measurement of samples taken by Greenpeace from the seabed at the end of the Sellafield discharge pipe are consistent with published results from the competent authority.

The concentrations of activity per unit mass in these samples indeed exceed the exemption values laid down in the Basic Safety Standards Directive (96/29/EURATOM) for two radionuclides (Caesium−137 and Americium−241). The Directive is however explicit with regard to materials contaminated as a result of authorized releases: Article 3.2(f) states that practices involving such materials do not need to be reported. This is of course without prejudice to the role of the authorities in ensuring that levels of radioactivity in the environment are monitored and that the resulting doses to the public are of no concern from the point of view of health.

The reporting requirement pertains to the intention to carry out a practice or any other action within the scope of this Directive. Practices carried out at the Sellafield site are reported to the UK authorities and are subject to prior authorization. Consequently there is no question of secret operations. The Commission is not aware of any concern by the UK authorities in relation to the contamination levels found.

There being no evidence of any breach of the Basic Safety Standards, any action prompted by the Greenpeace findings is a matter for the competent national authority.

The management of high level nuclear waste in the overall context of reprocessing is not related to the above issue. Regarding the management of such wastes and the improvement of the regulation of nuclear installations, the Commission will continue to seek improvements to standards of radiation protection, where practicable and where appropriate.

Question No 91 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−0560/98)

Subject: Safety of radioactive residues and waste from the Kozloduy power station in Bulgaria:

In view of the constant apprehension felt by the Greek public over the safety of the Kozloduy nuclear power station in Bulgaria and the fact that the Commission avoided answering my previous question (paragraph 4 in question E 940/98) I should appreciate answers to the following questions.

Where are the radioactive wastes and residues from the power station being safely disposed of and entombed?

By what means are they being transported and precisely where, within and outside Bulgaria?

Is there any contact between them, in particular the liquid waste from the power station, and the Bulgarian river system?

Are the safety rules and levels for atmospheric emissions from the ventilation system being complied with?

Has there been any reactor accidents or malfunctions recently, and how do the results of the latest soil, water and air measurements match up to the permitted safety levels?

Answer

The radioactive wastes are currently being temporarily stored on the site of the Kozloduy power station. A new processing, packaging and storage plant is under construction on the site of the Kozloduy power station.

Institutional radioactive waste is stored at the Novi‐Han site.

Transportation is consequently only taking place within the compound of the Kozloduy power station. Similarly, irradiated fuel has not been transported outside the power station since 1989.

Consequently, with regard to cross‐border transportation, no transportation of radioactive wastes is taking place between Bulgaria and other countries..

Nevertheless, following a multilateral agreement between four countries, including Russia, and according to the results of ongoing contractual discussions, exhausted fuel from the VVER 440 power stations (units 1 to 4) will in principle be sent back to Russia for reprocessing. The resulting high activity waste will have to return subsequently to Bulgaria for permanent storage. The VVER 1000 (units 5 and 6) fuel which is not reprocessed in Russia will remain permanently in Bulgaria.

Furthermore, liquid waste is stored on site after processing. The amount of liquid waste stored on site is approximately 7000 m3 . Nuclear activity is governed by a 1985 act on the use of nuclear energy for specific purposes.

The Commission has no authority in the area of control of gas or liquid emissions, nor in the area of rates of radioactivity in the environment outside Community territory.

Subject: Non‐compliance in the implementation of European projects in Albaizyn (Granada)

In line with the requirements of the grant offer, the project manager submitted an Annual Report on activities up to December 1997. The Commission has recently been informed that a new project manager has been appointed and a new management structure established to implement the approved actions. Further details on financial progress are to be submitted to the Commission prior to the next reporting date of 30 June 1998.

Question No 94 by Jean Querbes (H−0565/98)

Subject: Boosting resin production in Europe

At a meeting in Segovia on 6 January 1998 European representatives of the resin tapping industry adopted a charter with the aim of boosting resin production in Europe in order to make up the shortfall in the Community and contributing to job creation and the development of forests. Is the Commission willing, in its proposals for a new forestry strategy, to take account of the suggestions put forward in the Segovia Charter and introduce a specific plan to assist resin production in the European Union?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the significance of pine resin tapping in the support of forestry activity in certain areas of the Community, and of the advantages ensuing from it for forest conservation, employment and support of the rural fabric. It has taken note of the proposals for a revival of this activity contained in the “Segovia Charter’ which has been passed on to it.

The Commission considers that the measures provided for in the new draft Regulation on rural development, which will be operative during the period 2000‐2006, will constitute an appropriate framework for the implementation of support actions to the sector. The measures authorized by this Regulation will be applied horizontally across all the rural zones of the Community and will be implemented in a decentralized manner, at an appropriate level, on the initiative of Member States. It will be for the national, regional or local authorities to include actions aimed at reviving resin production in their programmes.

For this reason, the Commission does not feel it necessary to put a specific plan for aid to this sector in place.

Question No 95 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0570/98)

Subject: Overfishing of salmon in the Baltic

The overfishing of salmon in the Baltic must be stopped. The Swedish Government and Ministry of Agriculture must change their stance on drift‐net fishing for salmon in the Baltic. The wild salmon is under severe threat. It is estimated that only 7 % of the salmon in the Baltic at this time are wild. In Norrland, angling is extremely important for the resident population, tourism and the regional economy. Angling is also prudent with natural resources. Angling accounts for less than 1 % of the total annual salmon catch in the Baltic. Over‐exploitation is caused by large‐scale industrial fishing with 21 km drift‐nets. Restricting and banning angling in certain rivers with EU approval, as has hitherto been the case, is only of symbolic significance while industrial fishing with 21 km long drift‐nets is still allowed. If our wild salmon species are to survive and biological diversity preserved, drift‐net fishing in the Baltic must cease. It is important in future EU negotiations to persuade other countries around the Baltic to preserve the wild salmon and other biological diversity in the Baltic Sea on a sustainable basis. Does the Commission think that it should consider taking action on this issue?

Answer

The Commission shares the honourable Member's concern for the wild salmon stocks in the Baltic. In order to safeguard the wild salmon the Community took the initiative to develop the ‘Salmon Action Plan 1997‐2010’ that was adopted by the International Baltic Sea Fishery Commission (IBSFC) in February 1997. Amongst the objectives of the plan are to achieve a natural production of at least 50 % of the potential capacity of wild salmon rivers by the year 2010, and to shift the harvest from the open sea towards coastal areas and rivers.

In order to meet these objectives, all fisheries in rivers, coastal waters as well as in the open sea, need to be considered. For that purpose, the TAC for the open sea fisheries was gradually reduced to half of the previous levels, and a moratorium for fisheries in rivers and river mouths with wild salmon was declared. Measurements, recommended by IBSFC for the Convention Area, were supplemented by national measures.

Results can already be assessed: a steep increase in the return of wild salmon spawners that has resulted in an important rise in the number of offspring. This is positive evidence of the fact that the ‘IBSFC Salmon Action Plan’ is effective. However the Commission will continue to follow the situation and propose improvements in order to meet the objectives, if required.

During the Fisheries Council of 8 June 1998, it has been decided not to include the Baltic salmon fisheries within the driftnet ban which will become fully mandatory by the year 2002. But the Commission has confirmed that it will pay special attention to the evolution of the salmon fisheries in the Baltic.

Question No 96 by Niall Andrews (H−0577/98)

Subject: Child labour

How does the Commission intend to respond to the head of the ILO who has pledged to work to ensure that governments around the world back a new convention aimed at eliminating the worst forms of child labour.

Answer

The Commission is following very closely the work of the International Labour Conference of the ILO on a draft convention and recommendation designed to eliminate the worst forms of child labour. The Commission is taking part in this work as an observer.

The Commission hopes that this work will reach an optional conclusion in order to allow these instruments to be adopted at the next International Labour Conference in June 1999.

Since June 1997 the Commission has been drawing up a working document for the Council listing all the aspects of Union activity directed against child labour, in particular:

‐ regarding existing Community legislation on the protection of young people at work and more specifically the Council directive of June 1994 on the protection of young workers. This directive includes in its first article a general ban on child labour (any young person under the age of 15 or still at compulsory education age); the same article includes an obligation to protect young people (up to 18) against economic exploitation and any work liable to damage their health or physical, psychological, moral or social development or to jeopardize their education;

‐ the initiatives adopted against the sexual exploitation of children (Daphne and ESTOP programmes, joint action of February 1997...);

‐ regarding external relations, the insertion of clauses relating to the respect of democratic principles and human rights in all agreements with third countries, and the adoption by the Council of regulations introducing an incentive clause in the framework of GSP linked to the protection of workers' and children's rights.

Question No 97 by Gerard Collins (H−0578/98)

Subject: Poor implementation of UN money‐laundering Conventions

One of the central planks of UN action in the fight against drugs is the 1988 Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances which has still to be signed by 40 of the UN's 185 members.

However, since less that 30 of the countries signed up to the Convention are actually implementing anti‐money‐laundering measures substantially in line with the Convention, what does the Commission consider should be done to dramatically improve the implementation record of this Convention and would the Commission support punitive actions by the EU against the countries which fall far short of their obligations?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the fact that several countries that are party to the 1998 Convention against illicit traffic in narcotic drugs and psychotropic substances have still to implement national measures relating to the anti‐money laundering aspects of this Convention.

Such measures have also been examined in the framework of the Special Session of the General Assembly on international drug control of 8‐10 June 1998. The General Assembly adopted a specific text on combating money laundering, mentioning the need to develop international cooperation and for States to draw up and apply domestic legislation and to take law enforcement measures. This text calls also for the establishment of an effective financial and regulatory regime to deny criminals and their illicit funds access to national and international financial systems.

The Commission and all the EU Member States are members and firm supporters of the Financial Action Task Force on money laundering (FATF), the only international body dedicated solely to combating money laundering. The recent Birmingham G7 Summit endorsed the decision of the FATF to extend its mandate for a further five years and agreed that the major task during this period should be the establishment of a world‐wide anti‐money laundering network encompassing all continents and regions of the world.

The Commission is seeking to give active support to that strategy wherever possible and to encourage the adoption and implementation of anti‐money laundering measures. The Commission's efforts in this area in a number of regions are described in its second report on money laundering, which will very shortly be presented to Parliament and the Council.

Question No 98 by Mark Killilea (H−0580/98)

Subject: National secondary routes in Ireland

The Operational Programme for Transport 1994‐1999 makes available £1, 100 million pounds for developing national and primary routes and £114 million pounds for developing national secondary routes in Ireland.

In light of the fact that secondary routes play such an important role in developing local economies in rural Ireland, does the European Commission feel that greater resources should be made available under the next Operational Programme for Transport 2000‐2006 in developing such national secondary routes in Ireland?

Answer

The Community Support Framework for Ireland sets the priorities for structural funding during the period 1994‐1999. The MidTerm Review of this Framework carried out during 1997 confirmed the priority attributed to investments in national primary roads in the Transport Operational Programme. Despite the large investment in these roads co‐funded by the European Regional Development Fund and the Cohesion Fund the Mid‐Term review also showed that there is evidence of increased congestion. Given the relative shortage of road infrastructure in Ireland, it is to be expected that in the next programming period, investment in roads will continue to be a priority in the transport field. During the next period of structural funding for Ireland (2000‐2006) investment in roads will no doubt be a priority. However, it is too early to give an indication of the amount and of the relative priority between national primary roads and secondary routes.

Question No 99 by Liam Hyland (H−0581/98)

Subject: Benzene contamination in mineral drinks

As the Commission is undoubtedly aware benzene contamination led to more than 2 m cases of soft drink and thousands of bottles of carbonated mineral water being removed from the UK market on 1 June this year.

Can the Commission allay public anxiety about the use of benzene in soft drinks, including mineral waters, and does it consider that new guidelines need to be issued which ban or more strictly control the use of such substances in these drinks?

Answer

After receiving Mr Hyland's question the Commission asked for information from the British authorities and the professional circles concerned. It obtained the following information.

The contamination in the United Kingdom of natural mineral water, bottled drinking water and refreshing drinks by benzene was caused by carbon dioxide (CO2 ) added to these products. Following an accident in the production process carried out by the carbon dioxide supplier, the latter gas contained up to 2.3 ppm (parts per million) of benzene which led to contamination of the water and drinks under consideration of the order or 10 ppb (parts per billion).

A natural mineral water purveyor carrying out one of his regular self‐imposed checks discovered that the product was contaminated by benzene.

Once they had been informed of the problem the suppliers concerned called in the products which were likely to be contaminated. These products did not present any risk for human health given the very low rate of contamination.

The addition of benzene to the drinks concerned is prohibited. The public at large therefore has no reason to be anxious as the Honourable Member fears they might be. A Commission directive (96/77/EC) lays down the purity criteria for carbon dioxide (and many other substances) used in foodstuffs. No tolerance is laid down for benzene.

Question No 100 by James Fitzsimons (H−0582/98)

Subject: Proposed European Commission Office in Grange, Co. Meath

Can the Commission indicate when the offices at Grange, Co. Meath, are due to be occupied by the Commission staff dealing with veterinary and animal health issues and is the Commission satisfied with the rate of progress in organizing the transfer of staff to this location?

Question No 101 by Brian Crowley (H−0588/98)

Subject: Landfill sites

What proposals does the Commission have to control and reduce the environmental nuisance caused by landfill sites and also to encourage the use of alternative methods of waste disposal such as recycling?

Answer

In March 1997 the Commission adopted a Proposal for a Council Directive on the landfill of waste(8) . The European Parliament held a first hearing on this Proposal in February 1998. On 4 June 1998 the Council of Environment Ministers adopted a Common Position on the Proposal.

The objectives of the new Proposal are to prevent and reduce the adverse effects of existing and new landfills on the environment, in particular the pollution of surface water, ground water, soil and air, as well as the resulting risks to human health. The Proposal also seeks to harmonize the environmental and technical standards for the landfill of waste. To achieve these objectives, procedures are provided for controlling the opening, management, closure and monitoring of a site and the acceptability of the waste in the landfill.

In order to reduce the volume or hazardous nature of the waste, the Proposal requires all waste to be treated before land filling. To ensure high environmental protection during handling and control of waste going to landfills, the Proposals identifies waste specific landfills. Hereby, the Proposal ensures that landfills for non‐hazardous waste are used only for non‐hazardous waste, landfills for hazardous waste are used only for hazardous waste and landfill for inert waste are used only for inert waste.

In particular, with the aim of reducing the ‘global greenhouse effect’, the Proposal includes provisions to reduce the land filling of biodegradable municipal waste as well as provisions to ensure that the gases produced in new as well as existing landfills, which receives biodegradable waste, are collected, treated and used. The measures taken to prevent and reduce the land filling of biodegradable waste also aims at encouraging recovery and recycling of biodegradable waste (composting, biogas production and recycling of paper).

In order to encourage particularly composting, the Commission is at present preparing a Proposal on composting and biogas production. The Proposal is scheduled for 1999.

As regards recycling in general, the Commission is preparing a Communication on the competitiveness of the recycling industries. This Communication, which is expected to be adopted in 1998, also includes proposals for initiatives, which aim to encourage and promote the recycling of waste.

More specifically, the Commission adopted in July 1997 a Proposal for a Council Directive on end of life vehicles. This Proposal, which includes provisions for the recycling of end of life vehicles is at present being discussed in the European Parliament.

Furthermore, a new Proposal on electrical and electronic waste is currently under preparation as is a revision of the existing Directives on batteries, used oils and sewage sludge. These initiatives, which are expected to be adopted by the Commission at the end of 1998 or the beginning of 1999, will introduce provisions for the recycling of electrical and electronic waste, batteries, used oils and sewage sludge.

Question No 102 by Gerhard Hager (H−0589/98)

Subject: Protection of the euro against counterfeiting

The preparations for the introduction of the euro are in full swing and have already been completed in most respects. According to press reports, there are doubts about the protection of the new banknotes against counterfeiting.

What precautions have been taken by the EU to ensure the protection of euro notes and coins, and on what legal basis?

Answer

In compliance with Article 109 f 3 of the EC Treaty, the supervision of the technical preparation of euro banknotes has been undertaken by the European Monetary Institute. The final design and specifications of the banknotes agreed by the EMI Council include a wide range of security features, some of which are intended for the benefit of the general public and others are to be used for authentication purposes in banknote sorting and accepting machines. In addition, with a view to increasing efficiency in combatting counterfeiting, the EMI Council has agreed to set up a joint investigation centre and a database in which all data on counterfeit euro banknotes will be stored.

As far as coins are concerned, their issue is the competence of Member States. However, according to Article 105 a 2 of the EC Treaty, the Council may adopt measures to harmonise the denomination and technical specifications of all coins intended for circulation. On 3 May 1998 the Council adopted a regulation setting out the denominations and technical specification of euro coins. Those specifications include several security features (such as bicolour techniques, edge lettering, use of three layer materials) both for the benefit of the general public and of automatic machines. The possibility of creation of an investigation centre and counterfeit database for coins is being examined by the Member States.

Furthermore, at its meeting last 19 May, the Ecofin Council highlighted the importance of assuring the establishment of an effective protection system throughout the Economic and Monetary Union, from before the introduction of the euro notes and coins. The role which Europol will have to play in this regard was underlined. The Council, which took note of the work undertaken by the Commission in the protection of monetary union and the euro, asked the Commission to continue its initiatives with the aim of facilitating the exchange of information and the establishment of close and regular cooperation aimed at the prevention of, and fight against, counterfeiting based on respect for the principle of subsidiarity and the sharing of responsibilities as set out in the Maastricht Treaty.

The Commission will adopt a communication moving in this direction in the coming weeks.

Question No 103 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0593/98)

Subject: Retirement of workers in the tobacco industry

Workers in the tobacco industry are faced with the drastic consequences of Community legislation on tobacco production and quotas in Greece. Apart from employment problems, the 13 000 workers in the industry also have a retirement problem in that they require more days of paid employment than those available to them before retirement age in order to establish a pension entitlement. This problem is even more acute for elderly workers, who are not being taken on, particularly at a time of production cutbacks as a result of the quotas.

What measures will the Commission take to tackle this serious problem which derives directly from Community legislation concerning tobacco production in Greece and, it would seem, in other Member States?

Answer

I would like to reassure the honourable Member that the Commission is proposing no reduction in tobacco production quotas for Greece, or for any other Member State.

The Commission"s proposal consists of offering, to those producers who voluntarily choose to leave the sector only, compensation in the form of buying back their quotas. It thus aims to create a “security net’ for producers in difficulties.

Question No 104 by Robert Sturdy (H−0594/98)

Subject: The UK 'Born‐After' Date‐Based Export Scheme

Will the Commission please provide us with an update as to the progress that has been made toward approving the United Kingdom's 'Born‐After' Date‐Based Export Scheme, which was submitted to the Commission in October 1997?

Answer

Following evaluation of the United Kingdom submission by the Scientific Steering Committee, and after a series of discussions between the Commission and the British authorities, on 10 June the Commission approved a proposal to introduce a date‐based export scheme in the United Kingdom.

This proposal is now in the process of being discussed by the Member States in the Standing Veterinary Committee.

Question No 105 by Christine Oddy (H−0602/98)

Subject: Bhutanese refugees

Is the European Commission aware that 94 000 refugees are living in UNHCR – administered camps in Eastern Nepal and an estimated 10 000 are living elsewhere in Nepal and India?

Has the Commission seen the UK Refugee Council's recommendations to alleviate this situation? Will the Commission endorse these recommendations?

Answer

The Commission is indeed well aware that more than 90, 000 Bhutanese refugees are living in UNHCR administered camps in Eastern Nepal and that an estimated further 10, 000 are living elsewhere in Nepal and India. The Commission's knowledge is not surprising since it is the largest single donor to the support of these camps.

However, the Commission has not officially been informed of the UK Refugee Council's recommendation since it has not formally been presented to it. The Commission understands, however, that this recommendation has been addressed to the UK Government. In the absence of any direct appeal, the Commission cannot, therefore, take any position on it because obviously it would have to examine in detail any such request.
